<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Scythians</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">"Scythian" redirects here. For the obsolete stratigraphic term, see <a href="/wiki/Early_Triassic" title="Early Triassic">Early Triassic</a>. For the languages, see <a href="/wiki/Scythian_languages" title="Scythian languages">Scythian languages</a>. For the band, see <a href="/wiki/Scythian_(band)" title="Scythian (band)">Scythian (band)</a>.</div>

The central lower tier shows three horses, each being torn apart by two <a href="/wiki/Griffin" title="Griffin">griffins</a>.</div>

<ul>

</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Scythians</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="/θ/ 'th' in 'thigh'">θ</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/./ syllable break">.</span><span title="/ən/ 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="/ð/ 'th' in 'thy'">ð</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/./ syllable break">.</span><span title="/ən/ 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>/</a></span></span>; from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Σκύθης, Σκύθοι</span>), also known as <b>Scyth</b>, <b><a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a></b>, <b>Sakae</b>, <b>Sacae</b>, <b>Sai</b>, <b>Iskuzai</b>, or <b>Askuzai</b>, were a large group of <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBScythian_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBScythian-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WM_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-WM-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-West-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sinor97_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sinor97-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_nomads" title="Eurasian nomads">Eurasian nomads</a> who were mentioned by the literate peoples surrounding them as inhabiting large areas in the central <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurasian_steppe" title="Eurasian steppe">steppes</a> from about the 9th century BC until about the 1st century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Scythian_languages" title="Scythian languages">Scythian languages</a> belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Iranian_languages" title="Eastern Iranian languages">Eastern</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith61_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith61-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encarta2008_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-encarta2008-7">[7]</a></sup> The "classical Scythians" known to <a href="/wiki/Greek_historiography" title="Greek historiography">ancient Greek historians</a> were located in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontic_steppe" title="Pontic steppe">northern Black Sea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fore-Caucasus" title="Fore-Caucasus">fore-Caucasus</a> region. Other Scythian groups documented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Empire" title="Assyrian Empire">Assyrian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid" title="Achaemenid">Achaemenid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_China" title="Han China">Chinese</a> sources show that they also existed in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, where they were referred to as the <i>Iskuzai</i>/<i>Askuzai</i>, <i>Saka</i> (<a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a>: <i>Sakā</i>; <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">New Persian</a>: <b>ساکا</b>; <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Σάκαι</span>; <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>: <span lang="hy" xml:lang="hy">սկյութները</span>; <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>: <i>Sacae</i>, <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa" xml:lang="sa">शक</span> <i>Śaka</i>), and <i>Sai</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">塞</span>; <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a>: *<i><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">sˤək</span></i>), respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Although ancient Persian sources also used Saka to refer to the Western Scythians of the northern Black Sea, modern scholars usually use the term <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a> to refer to Iranian-speaking tribes who inhabited the Eastern Steppe and the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beckwith_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-beckwith-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L._T._Yablonsky_383_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-L._T._Yablonsky_383-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The relationships between the peoples living in these widely separated regions remains unclear. The term "Scythian" is used by modern scholars in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological_context" title="Archaeological context">archaeological context</a> for finds perceived to display attributes of the "Scytho-Siberian" culture, usually without implying an ethnic or linguistic connotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DavisKimball1995_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-DavisKimball1995-11">[11]</a></sup> The term <b>Scythic</b> may also be used in a similar way,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> "to describe a special phase that followed the widespread diffusion of mounted nomadism, characterized by the presence of special weapons, <a href="/wiki/Horse_tack" title="Horse tack">horse gear</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Animal_style" title="Animal style">animal art</a> in the form of metal plaques".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Their westernmost territories during the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> were known to classical Greek sources as <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythia</a>.</p>
<p>The Scythians were among the earliest peoples to master <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_warfare" title="Horses in warfare">mounted warfare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBScythian_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-EBScythian-1">[1]</a></sup> In the 8th century BC they possibly raided <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBThe_Steppe_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBThe_Steppe-14">[14]</a></sup> Soon after they expanded westwards and dislodged the <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a> from power on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontic_Steppe" title="Pontic Steppe">Pontic Steppe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBCentral_Asia_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBCentral_Asia-15">[15]</a></sup> At their peak, Scythians came to dominate the entire steppe zone,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith117_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith117-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith377_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith377-17">[17]</a></sup> stretching from the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a> in the west to central <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ordos_culture" title="Ordos culture">Ordos culture</a>) and the south <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tagar_culture" title="Tagar culture">Tagar culture</a>) in the east,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DavisKimball1995_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-DavisKimball1995-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> creating what has been referred to as the first Central Asian <a href="/wiki/Nomadic_empire" title="Nomadic empire">nomadic empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBCentral_Asia_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-EBCentral_Asia-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith11_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith11-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Based in what is modern-day <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, Southern European <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>, the western Scythians were ruled by a wealthy class known as the <b>Royal Scyths</b>. The Scythians established and controlled <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">a vast trade network</a> connecting <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Persia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_India" title="Ancient India">India</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">China</a>, perhaps contributing to the contemporary flourishing of those civilizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith58_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith58-20">[20]</a></sup> Settled metalworkers made portable decorative objects for the Scythians. These objects survive mainly in metal, forming a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Scythian_art" title="Scythian art">Scythian art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBArt_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBArt-21">[21]</a></sup> In the 7th century BC the Scythians crossed the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and frequently raided the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> along with the Cimmerians, playing an important role in the political developments of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBCentral_Asia_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-EBCentral_Asia-15">[15]</a></sup> Around 650–630 BC, Scythians briefly dominated the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> of the western <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBIran_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBIran-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith49_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith49-23">[23]</a></sup> stretching their power all the way to the borders of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBScythian_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-EBScythian-1">[1]</a></sup> After losing control over Media the Scythians continued intervening in Middle Eastern affairs, playing a leading role in the destruction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Empire" title="Assyrian Empire">Assyrian Empire</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nineveh_(612_BC)" title="Battle of Nineveh (612 BC)">Sack of Nineveh</a> in 612 BC. The Scythians subsequently engaged in frequent conflicts with the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>. The western Scythians suffered a major defeat against <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a> in the 4th century BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBScythian_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-EBScythian-1">[1]</a></sup> and were subsequently gradually conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a>, a related Iranian people from Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBSarmatian_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBSarmatian-24">[24]</a></sup> The Eastern Scythians of the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Steppe" title="Asian Steppe">Asian Steppe</a> (Saka) were attacked by the <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wusun" title="Wusun">Wusun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> in the 2nd century BC, prompting many of them to migrate into <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benjamin_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benjamin-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChineseHistory_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChineseHistory-26">[26]</a></sup> where they became known as <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith85_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith85-27">[27]</a></sup> At some point, perhaps as late as the 3rd century AD after the demise of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> and the Xiongnu, Eastern Scythians crossed the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a> and settled in the western <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a>, where the Scythian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotanese_language" title="Khotanese language">Khotanese</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumshuqese_language" title="Tumshuqese language">Tumshuqese languages</a> are attested in <a href="/wiki/Brahmi_script" title="Brahmi script">Brahmi scripture</a> from the 10th and 11th centuries AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChineseHistory_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChineseHistory-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, by the early Medieval Ages, the Scythians and their closely related Sarmatians were eventually assimilated and absorbed (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Slavicisation" title="Slavicisation">Slavicisation</a>) by the <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">Proto</a>-<a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavic</a> population of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Sarmatians.2C_600_BC-AD_450_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Sarmatians.2C_600_BC-AD_450-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_.26_Francis_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_.26_Francis-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women_in_Russia_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Women_in_Russia-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slovene_Studies_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slovene_Studies-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scythians kept herds of horses, cattle, and sheep, lived in tent-covered wagons, and fought with bows and arrows on horseback. They developed a rich culture characterized by opulent tombs, fine metalwork, and a brilliant art style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encarta2008_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-encarta2008-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span></h2>
<p>Sulimirski views the <i>Histories</i> of <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> as the most important literary source relating to ancient Scyths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Herodotus provides a depiction that can be related to the results of archaeological research, but apparently knew little of the eastern part of Scythia. He did say that the ancient Persians called all the Scyths <i>Σάκαι</i> (Sacae, Herodotus 7.64). Their principal tribe, the "Royal Scyths", ruled the vast lands occupied by the nation as a whole (Herodotus 4.20), calling themselves <i>Σκώλοτοι</i> (Scōloti, Herodotus 4.6). <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Szemer%C3%A9nyi" title="Oswald Szemerényi">Oswald Szemerényi</a> devotes a thorough discussion to the etymologies of ancient ethnic words for the Scythians in his work "Four old Iranian ethnic names: Scythian – Skudra – Sogdian – Saka". In it, the names of Herodotus and the names of his title, except Saka, as well as many other words for "Scythian," such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Assyrian</a> <i>Aškuz</i> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>Skuthēs</i>, descend from *skeud-, an ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European" title="Indo-European">Indo-European</a> root meaning "propel, shoot" (cf. English shoot).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> *skud- is the zero-grade; that is, a variant in which the -e- is not present. The restored Scythian name is *Skuda (<a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archer</a>), which among the Pontic or Royal Scythians became *Skula, in which the d has been regularly replaced by an l.</p>
<p>Saka, on the other hand, Szemerényi relates to an Iranian <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verbal</a> root, sak-, "go, roam", and hypothesizes that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid" title="Achaemenid">Achaemenids</a> used "nomad" to refer to the northern tribes, rather than their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endonym" title="Endonym">endonym</a>. The name does appear somewhat further east than the Achaemenid Empire, as the Chinese knew the Asian Scythians as Sai (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese character</a>: 塞, Old Sinitic <i>*sək</i>). Whether they adopted the Achaemenid name, or "Saka" came to be an endonym, it is not clear. The modern region of <a href="/wiki/Sistan" title="Sistan">Sistan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sistan_and_Baluchestan_Province" title="Sistan and Baluchestan Province">eastern Iran</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> takes its name from the classical <a href="/wiki/Sakastan" title="Sakastan">Sakastan</a> ("land of the Sakas").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sakastan was not the only province of Scythian origin on the eastern margin of the Persian Empire. According to Szemerényi, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a> was named from the Skuda form. Starting from the names of the province given in <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> inscriptions, Sugda and Suguda, and the knowledge derived from Middle Sogdian that Old Persian -gd- applied to Sogdian was pronounced as voiced fricatives, -γδ-, Szemerényi arrives at *Suγδa as an Old Sogdian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endonym" title="Endonym">endonym</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Applying sound changes apparent in other Sogdian words and inherent in Indo-European he traces the development of *Suγδa from Skuda, "archer," as follows: Skuda &gt; *Sukuda by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaptyxis" title="Anaptyxis">anaptyxis</a> &gt; *Sukuδa &gt; *Sukδa (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syncope_(phonetics)" title="Syncope (phonetics)">syncope</a>) &gt; *Suγδa (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">assimilation</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_evidence">Literary evidence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_125.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1817" data-file-width="2186" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Roman_Empire_125.png/220px-Roman_Empire_125.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Roman_Empire_125.png/330px-Roman_Empire_125.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Roman_Empire_125.png/440px-Roman_Empire_125.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_125.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Roman Empire under <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> (ruled AD 117–138), showing the location of the Scythae Basilaei ("Royal Scyths") along the north shore of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Scythians first appeared in the historical record in the 8th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szemer.C3.A9nyi_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Szemer.C3.A9nyi-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> reported three contradictory versions as to the origins of the Scythians, but placed greatest faith in this version:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There is also another different story, now to be related, in which I am more inclined to put faith than in any other. It is that the wandering Scythians once dwelt in Asia, and there warred with the <a href="/wiki/Massagetae" title="Massagetae">Massagetae</a>, but with ill success; they therefore quitted their homes, crossed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Araxes" title="Araxes">Araxes</a>, and entered the land of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Crimea" title="History of Crimea">Cimmeria</a>.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Accounts by Herodotus of Scythian origins has been discounted recently; although his accounts of Scythian raiding activities contemporary to his writings have been deemed more reliable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Moreover, the term Scythian, like Cimmerian, was used to refer to a variety of groups from the Black Sea to southern Siberia and central Asia. "They were not a specific people", but rather variety of peoples "referred to at variety of times in history, and in several places, none of which was their original homeland."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The Bible includes a single reference to Scythians in Colossians 3:11, immediately after mentioning barbarians, possibly as an extreme example of a barbarian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology">Archaeology</span></h3>
<p>Modern interpretation of historical, archaeological and anthropological evidence has proposed two broad hypotheses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallory1989_p51-53_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mallory1989_p51-53-45">[45]</a></sup> The first, formerly more espoused by Soviet and then Russian researchers, roughly followed Herodotus' (third) account, holding that the Scythians were an Eastern Iranian group who arrived from <a href="/wiki/Inner_Asia" title="Inner Asia">Inner Asia</a>, i.e. from the area of Turkestan and western Siberia. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallory1989_p51-53_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mallory1989_p51-53-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Ghirshman and others the Scythian cultural complex emerged from local groups of the <a href="/wiki/Srubna_culture" title="Srubna culture">"Timber Grave" (or <i>Srubna</i>) culture</a> at the Black Sea coast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallory1989_p51-53_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mallory1989_p51-53-45">[45]</a></sup> although this is also associated with the <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a>. According to Dolukhanov this proposal is supported by anthropological evidence which has found that Scythian skulls are similar to preceding findings from the Timber Grave culture, and distinct from those of the Central Asian <i>Sacae</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Yet, according to Mallory the archaeological evidence is poor, and the Andronovo culture and "at least the eastern outliers of the Timber-grave culture" may be identified as Indo-Iranian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallory1989_p51-53_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mallory1989_p51-53-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ScythianGroups.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="462" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/ScythianGroups.png/220px-ScythianGroups.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/ScythianGroups.png/330px-ScythianGroups.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/ScythianGroups.png/440px-ScythianGroups.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ScythianGroups.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scythian and related archaeological groups in circum- Pontic region, c. 7th to 3rd centuries BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Others have further stressed that "Scythian" was a very broad term used by both ancient and modern scholars to describe a whole host of otherwise unrelated peoples sharing only certain similarities in lifestyle (nomadism), cultural practices and language. The 1st millennium BC ushered a period of unprecedented cultural and economic connectivity amongst disparate and wide-ranging communities. A mobile, broadly similar lifestyle would have facilitated contacts amongst disparate ethnic groupings along the expansive Eurasian steppe from the Danube to Manchuria, leading to many cultural similarities. From the viewpoint of Greek and Persian ancient observers, they were all lumped together under the <i>etic</i> category "Scythians".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h3>
<p>Early physical analyses have unanimously concluded that the Scythians, even those in the east (e.g. the Pazyryk region), possessed predominantly "Europioid" features, although mixed 'Euro-mongoloid" phenotypes also occur, depending on site and period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Numerous ancient <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> samples have now been recovered from Bronze and Iron Age communities in the Eurasian steppe and Siberian forest zone, the putative 'ancestors' of the historical Scythians. Compared to Y-DNA, mtDNA is easier to extract and amplify from ancient specimens due to numerous copies of mtDNA per cell.</p>
<p>The earliest studies could only analyze segments of mtDNA, thus providing only broad correlations of affinity to modern 'West Eurasian' or 'East Eurasian' populations. For example, a 2002 study, the mitochondrial DNA of Saka period male and female skeletal remains from a double inhumation <a href="/wiki/Kurgan" title="Kurgan">kurgan</a> at the Beral site in Kazakhstan was analysed. The two individuals were found to be not closely related. The HV1 mitochondrial sequence of the male was similar to the Anderson sequence which is most frequent in European populations. On the other hand, the HV1 sequence of the female suggested a greater likelihood of Asian origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>More recent studies have been able to type for specific <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">mtDNA lineages</a>. For example, a 2004 study studied the <a href="/wiki/Hypervariable_region" title="Hypervariable region">HV1 sequence</a> obtained from a male "Scytho-Siberian" at the Kizil site in the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Republic" title="Altai Republic">Altai Republic</a>. It belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_N_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup N (mtDNA)">N1a</a> maternal lineage, a geographically "west Eurasian lineage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Another study by the same team, again from two Scytho-Siberian skeletons found in the Altai Republic, were phenotypically males "of mixed Euro-Mongoloid origin". One of the individuals was found to carry the <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_F_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup F (mtDNA)">F2a</a> maternal lineage, and the other the <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_D_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup D (mtDNA)">D</a> lineage, both of which are characteristic of "East Eurasian" populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>These early studies have been elaborated by an increasing number of studies by Russian scholars. Conclusions which might be drawn thus far, from an mtDNA perspective, are (i) an early, Bronze Age mixture of both west and east Eurasian lineages, with western lineages being found far to the East, but not vice versa; (ii) an apparent reversal by Iron Age times, with increasing presence of East Eurasian lineages in the western steppe; (iii) the possible role of migrations from the sedentary south: the Balkano-Danubian and Iranian regions toward the steppe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ancient Y-DNA data was finally provided by Keyser <i>et al</i> in 2009. They studied the haplotypes and haplogroups of 26 ancient human specimens from the <a href="/wiki/Krasnoyarsk" title="Krasnoyarsk">Krasnoyarsk</a> area in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> dated from between the middle of the 2nd millennium BC and the 4th century AD (Scythian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarmatian" title="Sarmatian">Sarmatian</a> timeframe). Nearly all subjects belong to haplogroup <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_R-M17_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup R-M17 (Y-DNA)">R-M17</a>. The authors suggest that their data shows that between Bronze and Iron Ages the constellation of populations known variously as Scythians, Andronovians, etc. were blue- (or green-) eyed, fair-skinned and light-haired people who might have played a role in the early development of the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a> civilization. Moreover, this study found that they were genetically more closely related to modern populations of eastern Europe than those of central and southern Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The ubiquity and utter dominance of R1a Y-DNA lineage contrasts markedly with the diversity seen in the mtDNA profiles.</p>
<p>However, this comparison was made on the basis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short_tandem_repeats" title="Short tandem repeats">STRs</a>. Since the 2009 study by Keyser <i>et al</i>, population and geographic specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SNPs" title="SNPs">SNPs</a> have been discovered which can accurately distinguish between "European" R1a (<b>M458, Z280</b>) and "South Asian" R1a (<b>Z93</b>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Re-analyzing ancient Scytho-Siberian samples for these more specific subclades will further elucidate if the Eurasian steppe populations have an ultimate Eastern European or EurAsian origin, or perhaps, both. This, in turn, might also depend on which population is studied, i.e. Herodotus' European "classical' Scythians, the Central Asian <i>Sakae</i> or un-named nomadic groups in the far east (Altai region) who also bore a 'Scythian" cultural tradition.</p>
<p>In a study conducted in 2014 by VV Ilyinskyon on bone fragments from 10 Alanic burials on the Don River, DNA could be abstracted from a total of 7. 4 of them turned out as belonging to yDNA Haplogroup G2 and 6 of them had mtDNA I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015 the Institute of Archaeology in Moscow conducted researches on various Sarmatian-Alan and Saltovo-Mayaki culture Kurgan burials. In these analyses, the two Alan samples from 4th to 6th century AD turned out with yDNAs G2a-P15 and R1a-z94, while from the three Sarmatian samples from 2nd to 3rd century AD two turned out both with yDNA J1-M267 and one with R1a.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> And the three Saltovo-Mayaki samples from 8th to 9th century AD turned out with yDNAs G, J2a-M410 and R1a-z94 respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the recent two years new aDNA tests were made on various ancient samples across Eurasia, among them two from Scythian burials. This time the modern techniques of SNPs (in comparison to STRs in earlier tests) were in use. The Iron Age Scythian samples from the Volga region and European Steppes appear neither closely related to Eastern Europeans nor South- and Central Asians. Based on the results on professional as well amateur aDNA calculators both samples appear similar like a link between the Iranic speaking people of South-Central Asia as well people of the northern regions of West Asia on one side and Eastern Europeans on the other. This also fits fine with their geographic origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Antiquity_.28600_BC_to_AD_300.29">Classical Antiquity (600 BC to AD 300)</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Scythians&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(December 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avar%C3%A1rok.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Avar%C3%A1rok.JPG/180px-Avar%C3%A1rok.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Avar%C3%A1rok.JPG/270px-Avar%C3%A1rok.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Avar%C3%A1rok.JPG/360px-Avar%C3%A1rok.JPG 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avar%C3%A1rok.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scythian defence line 339 BC reconstruction in <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Polg%C3%A1r" title="Polgár">Polgár</a>, Hungary</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> provides the first detailed description of the Scythians. He classes the <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a> as a distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autochthon_(person)" title="Autochthon (person)">autochthonous</a> tribe, expelled by the Scythians from the northern Black Sea coast (<i>Hist.</i> 4.11–12). Herodotus also states (4.6) that the Scythians consisted of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Auchatae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Auchatae (page does not exist)">Auchatae</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Catiaroi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Catiaroi (page does not exist)">Catiaroi</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Traspians&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Traspians (page does not exist)">Traspians</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Paralatae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Paralatae (page does not exist)">Paralatae</a> or "Royal Scythians".</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>For Herodotus, the Scythians were outlandish barbarians living north of the Black Sea in what are now <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>, Ukraine and Crimea.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Michael Kulikowski, <i>Rome's Gothic Wars from the Third Century to Alaric</i>, pg. 14</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In 512 BC, when King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> of <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persia</a> <a href="/wiki/European_Scythian_campaign_of_Darius_I" title="European Scythian campaign of Darius I">attacked the Scythians</a>, he allegedly penetrated into their land after crossing the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>. Herodotus relates that the nomadic Scythians frustrated the Persian army by letting it march through the entire country without an engagement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene-63">[63]</a></sup> According to Herodotus, Darius in this manner came as far as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volga" title="Volga">Volga</a> River.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KulObaTreasure.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="839" data-file-width="907" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/KulObaTreasure.jpg/220px-KulObaTreasure.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/KulObaTreasure.jpg/330px-KulObaTreasure.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/KulObaTreasure.jpg/440px-KulObaTreasure.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KulObaTreasure.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Treasure of <a href="/wiki/Kul-Oba" title="Kul-Oba">Kul-Oba</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Kerch" title="Kerch">Kerch</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the 5th to 3rd centuries BC, the Scythians evidently prospered. When Herodotus wrote his <i>Histories</i> in the 5th century BC, Greeks distinguished <a href="/wiki/Scythia_Minor" title="Scythia Minor">Scythia Minor</a>, in present-day <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, from a <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Greater Scythia</a> that extended eastwards for a 20-day ride from the Danube River, across the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a> of today's East Ukraine to the lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_River,_Russia" title="Don River, Russia">Don</a> basin. The Don, then known as <i><a href="/wiki/Tanais" title="Tanais">Tanaïs</a></i>, has served as a major trading route ever since. The Scythians apparently obtained their wealth from their control over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a> from the north to Greece through the Greek <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apoikia" title="Apoikia">colonial ports</a> of <a href="/wiki/Olbia" title="Olbia">Olbia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chersonesos_Taurica" title="Chersonesos Taurica">Chersonesos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cimmerian_Bosporus" title="Cimmerian Bosporus">Cimmerian Bosporus</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gorgippia" title="Gorgippia">Gorgippia</a>. They also grew grain, and shipped wheat, flocks, and cheese to Greece.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scythian_Warriors.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="282" data-file-width="735" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Scythian_Warriors.jpg/320px-Scythian_Warriors.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Scythian_Warriors.jpg/480px-Scythian_Warriors.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Scythian_Warriors.jpg/640px-Scythian_Warriors.jpg 2x" width="320"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scythian_Warriors.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scythian warriors, drawn after figures on an <a href="/wiki/Electrum" title="Electrum">electrum</a> cup from the <a href="/wiki/Kul-Oba" title="Kul-Oba">Kul-Oba</a> <a href="/wiki/Kurgan" title="Kurgan">kurgan</a> burial near <a href="/wiki/Kerch" title="Kerch">Kerch</a>, Crimea. The warrior on the right strings his bow, bracing it behind his knee; note the typical pointed hood, long jacket with fur or fleece trimming at the edges, decorated trousers, and short boots tied at the ankle. Scythians apparently wore their hair long and loose, and all adult men apparently bearded. The <i>gorytos</i> appears clearly on the left hip of the bare-headed spearman. The shield of the central figure may be made of plain leather over a wooden or wicker base. (<a href="/wiki/Hermitage_Museum" title="Hermitage Museum">Hermitage Museum</a>, St Petersburg)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> (c. 63 BC – AD 24) reports that King <a href="/wiki/Ateas" title="Ateas">Ateas</a> united under his power the Scythian tribes living between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maeotian_marshes" title="Maeotian marshes">Maeotian marshes</a> and the Danube. His westward expansion brought him into conflict with <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a> (reigned 359 to 336 BC), who took military action against the Scythians in 339 BC. Ateas died in battle, and his empire disintegrated. In the aftermath of this defeat, the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a> seem to have displaced the Scythians from the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>; while in south Russia, a kindred tribe, the <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a>, gradually overwhelmed them. In 329 BC Philip's son, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, came into conflict with the Scythians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jaxartes" title="Battle of Jaxartes">Battle of Jaxartes</a>. A Scythian army sought to take revenge against the Macedonians for the death of Ateas, as they pushed the borders of their empire north and east, and to take advantage of a revolt by the local Sogdian <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a>. However, the Scythian army was defeated by Alexander at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jaxartes" title="Battle of Jaxartes">Battle of Jaxartes</a>. Alexander did not intend to subdue the nomads: he wanted to go to the south, where a far more serious crisis demanded his attention. He could do so now without loss of face; and in order to make the outcome acceptable to the Saccae, he released the Scythian prisoners of war without ransom in order to broker a peace agreement. This policy was successful, and the Scythians no longer harassed Alexander's empire. By the time of Strabo's account (the first decades AD), the Crimean Scythians had created a new kingdom extending from the lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dnieper_River" title="Dnieper River">Dnieper</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>. The kings <a href="/wiki/Skilurus" title="Skilurus">Skilurus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palakus" title="Palakus">Palakus</a> waged wars with <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_VI_of_Pontus" title="Mithridates VI of Pontus">Mithridates the Great</a> (reigned 120–63 BC) for control of the Crimean littoral, including Chersonesos Taurica and the Cimmerian Bosporus. Their capital city, <a href="/wiki/Scythian_Neapolis" title="Scythian Neapolis">Scythian Neapolis</a>, stood on the outskirts of modern <a href="/wiki/Simferopol" title="Simferopol">Simferopol</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a> destroyed it later, in the mid-3rd century AD.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sakas_of_the_Eastern_Steppe">Sakas of the Eastern Steppe</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ScythianC14AsiaEuropeFig6SketchEn_3dGraph.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="679" data-file-width="599" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/06/ScythianC14AsiaEuropeFig6SketchEn_3dGraph.gif/260px-ScythianC14AsiaEuropeFig6SketchEn_3dGraph.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/06/ScythianC14AsiaEuropeFig6SketchEn_3dGraph.gif/390px-ScythianC14AsiaEuropeFig6SketchEn_3dGraph.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/06/ScythianC14AsiaEuropeFig6SketchEn_3dGraph.gif/520px-ScythianC14AsiaEuropeFig6SketchEn_3dGraph.gif 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ScythianC14AsiaEuropeFig6SketchEn_3dGraph.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Timeline of Scythian kurgans in Asia and Europe.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Modern scholars usually use the term Saka to refer to Iranian-speaking tribes who inhabited the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Steppe" title="Asian Steppe">Eastern Steppe</a> and the Tarim Basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beckwith_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-beckwith-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L._T._Yablonsky_383_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-L._T._Yablonsky_383-10">[10]</a></sup> Ancient Persian inscriptions also used Saka to refer to western Scythians to the north of the Black Sea – the <i>Sakā paradraya</i> or "Saka beyond the sea".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gershevitch_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-gershevitch-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dandamayev_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-dandamayev-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Achaemenid-era <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> inscriptions found at <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, dated to the reign of Darius I (r. 522-486 BC), the Saka are said to have lived just beyond the borders of <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdiana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailey_1996_1230_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-bailey_1996_1230-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The term <i>Sakā para Sugdam</i> or "Saka beyond Sugda (<a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdiana</a>)" was used by Darius to describe the people who formed the limits of his empire at the opposite end to <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kush</a> (the Ethiopians) in the west, i.e. at the eastern edge of his empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gershevitch_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-gershevitch-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> An inscription dated to the reign of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> (r. 486-465 BC) has them coupled with the <a href="/wiki/Dahae" title="Dahae">Dahae</a> people of Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailey_1996_1230_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-bailey_1996_1230-66">[66]</a></sup> Two Saka tribes named in the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>, <i>Sakā tigraxaudā</i> ("Saka with pointy hats/caps") and the <i>Sakā haumavargā</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Haoma" title="Haoma">haoma</a>-drinking saka"), may be located to the east of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gershevitch_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-gershevitch-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Some argued that the <i>Sakā haumavargā</i> may be the <i>Sakā para Sugdam</i>, therefore <i>Sakā haumavargā</i> would be located further east than the <i>Sakā tigraxaudā</i>. Some argued for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pamirs" title="Pamirs">Pamirs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a> as their location, although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jaxartes" title="Jaxartes">Jaxartes</a> is considered to be their more likely location given that the name says "beyond Sogdiana" rather than <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gershevitch_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-gershevitch-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Behistun.Inscript.Skunkha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="250" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Behistun.Inscript.Skunkha.jpg/150px-Behistun.Inscript.Skunkha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Behistun.Inscript.Skunkha.jpg/225px-Behistun.Inscript.Skunkha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Behistun.Inscript.Skunkha.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Behistun.Inscript.Skunkha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Skunkha" title="Skunkha">Skunkha</a>, king of the <i>Sakā tigraxaudā</i> ("pointed-cap-wearing Sakae"). Detail of <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> of the Persian Achaemenid Empire fought the Saka whose women were said to fight alongside their men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eolss_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-eolss-71">[71]</a></sup> According to Herodotus, Cyrus the Great also confronted the <a href="/wiki/Massagetae" title="Massagetae">Massagetae</a>, a people thought to be related to the Saka,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> while campaigning to the east of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> and was killed in the battle in 530 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> also waged wars against the eastern Sakas, who fought him with three armies led by three kings according to <a href="/wiki/Polyaenus" title="Polyaenus">Polyaenus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In 520–519 BC, Darius I defeated the <i>Sakā tigraxaudā</i> tribe and captured their king <a href="/wiki/Skunkha" title="Skunkha">Skunkha</a> (depicted as wearing a pointed hat in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behistun_inscription" title="Behistun inscription">Behistun inscription</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beckwith_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-beckwith-9">[9]</a></sup> The territories of Saka were absorbed into the Achaemenid Empire as part of <a href="/wiki/Chorasmia_(satrapy)" title="Chorasmia (satrapy)">Chorasmia</a> that included much of <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Amu Darya</a> (Oxus) and <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Syr Darya</a> (Jaxartes),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and the Saka then supplied the Persian army with large number of mounted bowmen in the Achaemenid wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dandamayev_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-dandamayev-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Han" title="Book of Han">Book of Han</a></i>, the valleys of the <a href="/wiki/Ili_River" title="Ili River">Ili River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chu_River" title="Chu River">Chu River</a> were called the "land of the Sai", i.e. the Saka. The exact date of their arrival in this region of Central Asia is unclear, perhaps it was just before the reign of <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yu_2010_p13_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-yu_2010_p13-76">[76]</a></sup> Around 30 Saka tombs in the form of <a href="/wiki/Kurgan" title="Kurgan">kurgans</a> (burial mounds) have also been found in the <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a> area dated to between 550–250 BC. Indications of Saka presence have also been found in the Tarim Basin region, possibly as early as the 7th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mallory_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-mallory-77">[77]</a></sup> Some modern scholars thought that the sacking of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a> capital <a href="/wiki/Haojing" title="Haojing">Haojing</a> in 770 BC might have been connected to a Scythian raid from the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai</a> before their westward expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scythian_successes_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scythian_successes-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Issyk_Golden_Cataphract_Warrior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="751" data-file-width="283" height="531" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Issyk_Golden_Cataphract_Warrior.jpg/200px-Issyk_Golden_Cataphract_Warrior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Issyk_Golden_Cataphract_Warrior.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Issyk_Golden_Cataphract_Warrior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">cataphract</a>-style parade armour of a Saka royal, also known as "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Golden_Warrior&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Golden Warrior (page does not exist)">The Golden Warrior</a>", from the <a href="/wiki/Issyk_kurgan" title="Issyk kurgan">Issyk kurgan</a>, an historic burial near ex-capital city of <a href="/wiki/Almaty" title="Almaty">Almaty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, as a consequence of the fight for supremacy between the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> and other groups, the Saka were pushed towards Bactria, and later on southward to northwest India and eastward to the oasis city-states of western <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northwest_China" title="Northwest China">Northwest China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Accounts of the migration of the Sakas are given in Chinese texts such as <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiji" title="Shiji">Shiji</a></i>. The Indo-European <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a>, who originally lived between <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Qilian_Mountains" title="Qilian Mountains">Qilian Mountains</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>, China, were assaulted and forced to flee from the <a href="/wiki/Hexi_Corridor" title="Hexi Corridor">Hexi Corridor</a> of Gansu by the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Mongols" title="Proto-Mongols">Mongolic</a> forces of the Xiongnu ruler <a href="/wiki/Modu_Chanyu" title="Modu Chanyu">Modu Chanyu</a>, who conquered the area in 177-176 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In turn the Yuezhi were responsible for attacking and pushing the Sai (i.e. Saka) southwest into Sogdiana, where in the mid 2nd century BC the latter crossed the <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Syr Darya</a> into <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a>, but also into the <a href="/wiki/Fergana_Valley" title="Fergana Valley">Fergana Valley</a> where they settled in <a href="/wiki/Dayuan" title="Dayuan">Dayuan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The ancient Greco-Roman geographer <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> claims that the four tribes of the <a href="/wiki/Asii" title="Asii">Asii</a>, who took down the Bactrians in the Greek and Roman account, came from land north of Syr Darya where the Ili and Chu valleys are located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yu_2010_p13_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-yu_2010_p13-76">[76]</a></sup> The Saka then migrated down to the northwest area of the Indian subcontinent where they became known as <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythians</a>, as well as eastward to the settlements of the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a> in present-day China such as <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Khotan" title="Kingdom of Khotan">Khotan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tumxuk" title="Tumxuk">Tumshaq</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Khotan_and_kingdoms_of_the_Tarim_Basin">Khotan and kingdoms of the Tarim Basin</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Khotan" title="Kingdom of Khotan">Kingdom of Khotan</a></div>
<p>The Saka migrated from Bactria where they eventually settled in some of the oasis city-states of the Tarim Basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yu_2010_p13_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-yu_2010_p13-76">[76]</a></sup> These states in the Tarim Basin include <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Khotan" title="Kingdom of Khotan">Khotan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yarkant_County" title="Yarkant County">Shache</a> (莎車, probably named after the Saka inhabitants), Yanqi (焉耆, <a href="/wiki/Karasahr" title="Karasahr">Karasahr</a>) and Qiuci (龜茲, <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>The official administrative language of Khotan and nearby <a href="/wiki/Shanshan" title="Shanshan">Shanshan</a> was <a href="/wiki/G%C4%81ndh%C4%81r%C4%AB_language" title="Gāndhārī language">Gandhari Prakrit</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kharosthi" title="Kharosthi">Kharosthi</a> script,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emmerick_2003_p265_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-emmerick_2003_p265-88">[88]</a></sup> there are however indications that Sakas were linked to the ruling elite – 3rd-century documents from Shanshan record the title of the king of Khotan as <i>hinajha</i> (i.e. "generalissimo"), an Iranian-based word equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> title <i><a href="/wiki/Senapati" title="Senapati">senapati</a></i>, yet nearly identical to the Khotanese Saka <i>hīnāysa</i> attested in later documents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emmerick_2003_p265_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-emmerick_2003_p265-88">[88]</a></sup> The regnal periods were also given in Khotanese as <i>kṣuṇa</i>, "implies an established connection between the Iranian inhabitants and the royal power," according to the late Professor of Iranian Studies Ronald E. Emmerick (d. 2001).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emmerick_2003_p265_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-emmerick_2003_p265-88">[88]</a></sup> He contended that Khotanese-Saka-language royal rescripts of Khotan dated to the 10th century "makes it likely that the ruler of Khotan was a speaker of <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emmerick_2003_p265_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-emmerick_2003_p265-88">[88]</a></sup> Furthermore, he argued that the oldest form of the name of Khotan, <i>hvatana</i>, may be linked semantically with the name Saka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>During China's <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618-907 AD), the region once again came under Chinese <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> with the campaigns of conquest by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a> (r. 626-649).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> From the late 8th to 9th centuries, the region changed hands between the Chinese Tang Empire and the rival <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Empire" title="Tibetan Empire">Tibetan Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The kingdom existed until it was conquered by the Muslim <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic peoples</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate" title="Kara-Khanid Khanate">Kara-Khanid Khanate</a>, which led to both the <a href="/wiki/Turkification" title="Turkification">Turkification</a> and Islamization of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythians</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythians</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AzesIITriratna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="964" data-file-width="1962" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/AzesIITriratna.jpg/260px-AzesIITriratna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/AzesIITriratna.jpg/390px-AzesIITriratna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/AzesIITriratna.jpg/520px-AzesIITriratna.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AzesIITriratna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silver coin of Indo-Scythian King <a href="/wiki/Azes_II" title="Azes II">Azes II</a> (ruled c. 35–12 BC). Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Triratna" title="Triratna">triratna</a> symbol in the left field on the reverse.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the Saka migrated into northwest area of the Indian subcontinent, the region became known as "land of the Saka" (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Drangiana" title="Drangiana">Drangiana</a>, of modern Afghanistan and Pakistan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailey_1996_1230_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-bailey_1996_1230-66">[66]</a></sup> This is attested in a contemporary <a href="/wiki/Kharosthi" title="Kharosthi">Kharosthi</a> inscription found on the <a href="/wiki/Mathura_lion_capital" title="Mathura lion capital">Mathura lion capital</a> belonging to the Saka kingdom of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythians</a> (200 BC - 400 AD) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_India" title="Northern India">northern India</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailey_1996_pp1230-1231_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-bailey_1996_pp1230-1231-95">[95]</a></sup> roughly the same time the Chinese record that the Saka had invaded and settled the country of <i>Jibin</i> 罽賓 (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>, of modern-day India and Pakistan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theobald_2011_saka_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-theobald_2011_saka-96">[96]</a></sup> In the Persian language of contemporary Iran the territory of Drangiana was called Sakastāna, in Armenian as <a href="/wiki/Sakastan" title="Sakastan">Sakastan</a>, with similar equivalents in Pahlavi, Greek, Sogdian, Syriac, Arabic, and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a> tongue used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turfan" title="Turfan">Turfan</a>, Xinjiang, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailey_1996_pp1230-1231_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-bailey_1996_pp1230-1231-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>For more information on Indo-Scythians and other central and southern Asian nomadic groups, see <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythians</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Antiquity_.28AD_300_to_600.29">Late Antiquity (AD 300 to 600)</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, the notion of a Scythian ethnicity grew more vague and outsiders might dub any people inhabiting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontic-Caspian_steppe" title="Pontic-Caspian steppe">Pontic-Caspian steppe</a> as "Scythians", regardless of their language. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Priscus" title="Priscus">Priscus</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> emissary to <a href="/wiki/Attila" title="Attila">Attila</a>, repeatedly referred to the latter's followers as "Scythians". But <a href="/wiki/Eunapius" title="Eunapius">Eunapius</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudius_Cladianus" title="Claudius Cladianus">Claudius Cladianus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olympiodorus_of_Thebes" title="Olympiodorus of Thebes">Olympiodorus</a> usually mean "Goths" when they write "Scythians".</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a> had displaced the <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a> in the 2nd century from most areas near the Roman frontier, and by early medieval times, the <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">Early Slavs</a> (Proto-Slavs) marginalized Eastern Iranian dialects in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> as they assimilated and absorbed the Iranian ethnic groups in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Sarmatians.2C_600_BC-AD_450_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Sarmatians.2C_600_BC-AD_450-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_.26_Francis_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_.26_Francis-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women_in_Russia_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Women_in_Russia-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slovene_Studies_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Slovene_Studies-32">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Turkic_migration" title="Turkic migration">Turkic migration</a> assimilated the <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a> linguistically in Central Asia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology_2">Archaeology</span></h2>
<p>Archaeological remains of the Scythians include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurgans" title="Kurgans">kurgan</a> tombs (ranging from simple exemplars to elaborate "Royal kurgans" containing the "Scythian triad" of weapons, horse-harness, and Scythian-style wild-animal art), <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>, and animal sacrifices, in places also with suspected <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice" title="Human sacrifice">human sacrifices</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mummy" title="Mummy">Mummification</a> techniques and <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> have aided in the relative preservation of some remains. Scythian archaeology also examines the remains of North Pontic Scythian cities and fortifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>The spectacular Scythian grave-goods from Arzhan, and others in <a href="/wiki/Tuva" title="Tuva">Tuva</a> have been dated from about 900 BC onward. One grave find on the lower Volga gave a similar date, and one of the Steblev graves from the East European end of the Scythian area was dated to the late 8th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Archaeologists can distinguish three periods of ancient Scythian archaeological remains:</p>
<ul>
<li>1st period – pre-Scythian and initial Scythian epoch: from the 9th to the middle of the 7th century BC</li>
<li>2nd period – early Scythian epoch: from the 7th to the 6th centuries BC</li>
<li>3rd period – classical Scythian epoch: from the 5th to the 4th centuries BC</li>
</ul>
<p>From the 8th to the 2nd centuries BC, archaeology records a split into two distinct settlement areas: the older in the Sayan-Altai area in Central Asia, and the younger in the North Pontic area in Eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kurgans">Kurgans</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kurgan" title="Kurgan">Kurgan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Throne_arm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="509" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e0/Throne_arm.jpg/220px-Throne_arm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e0/Throne_arm.jpg/330px-Throne_arm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e0/Throne_arm.jpg/440px-Throne_arm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Throne_arm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An arm from the throne of a Scythian king, 7th century BC. Found at the Kerkemess kurgan, <a href="/wiki/Krasnodar_Krai" title="Krasnodar Krai">Krasnodar Krai</a> in 1905. On exhibit at the <a href="/wiki/Hermitage_Museum" title="Hermitage Museum">Hermitage Museum</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>These large burial mounds (some over 20 metres high) provide the most valuable archaeological remains associated with the Scythians. They dot the Eurasian steppe belt, from Mongolia to Balkans, through Ukrainian and south Russian steppes, extending in great chains for many kilometers along ridges and watersheds. From them archaeologists have learned much about Scythian life and art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-102">[102]</a></sup> Some Scythian tombs reveal traces of Greek, Chinese, and Indian craftsmanship, suggesting a process of <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinification" title="Sinification">Sinification</a>, and other local influences among the Scythians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Ukrainian term for such a burial mound, <i>kurhán</i> (Ukrainian: Курган) as well as the Russian term <i>kurgán</i>, derives from a <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a> word for "castle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some Scythian-Sarmatian cultures may have given rise to Greek stories of <a href="/wiki/Amazons" title="Amazons">Amazons</a>. Graves of armed females have been found in southern Ukraine and Russia. David Anthony notes, "About 20% of Scythian-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarmatian" title="Sarmatian">Sarmatian</a> "warrior graves" on the lower <a href="/wiki/Don_River_(Russia)" title="Don River (Russia)">Don</a> and lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volga_river" title="Volga river">Volga</a> contained females dressed for battle as if they were men, a style that may have inspired the Greek tales about the Amazons."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anthonyd_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-anthonyd-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Excavation at kurgan Sengileevskoe-2 found gold bowls with coatings indicating a strong opium beverage was used while cannabis was burning nearby. The gold bowls depicted scenes showing clothing and weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pazyryk_culture">Pazyryk culture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PazyrikHorseman.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/PazyrikHorseman.JPG/220px-PazyrikHorseman.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/PazyrikHorseman.JPG/330px-PazyrikHorseman.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/PazyrikHorseman.JPG/440px-PazyrikHorseman.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PazyrikHorseman.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <i><a href="/wiki/Pazyryk_culture" title="Pazyryk culture">Pazyryk</a></i> horseman in a felt painting from a burial around 300 BC. The Pazyryks appear to be closely related to the Scythians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parragon_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parragon-107">[107]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pazyryk_culture" title="Pazyryk culture">Pazyryk culture</a></div>
<p>Some of the first <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> Scythian burials documented by modern archaeologists include the <a href="/wiki/Kurgan" title="Kurgan">kurgans</a> at <a href="/wiki/Pazyryk_burials" title="Pazyryk burials">Pazyryk</a> in the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ulagan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ulagan (page does not exist)">Ulagan</a> (Red) district of the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Republic" title="Altai Republic">Altai Republic</a>, south of <a href="/wiki/Novosibirsk" title="Novosibirsk">Novosibirsk</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a> of southern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> (near Mongolia). Archaeologists have extrapolated the <a href="/wiki/Pazyryk_culture" title="Pazyryk culture">Pazyryk culture</a> from these finds: five large burial mounds and several smaller ones between 1925 and 1949, one opened in 1947 by Russian archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Rudenko" title="Sergei Rudenko">Sergei Rudenko</a>. The burial mounds concealed chambers of larch-logs covered over with large <a href="/wiki/Cairn" title="Cairn">cairns</a> of boulders and stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Pazyryk culture flourished between the 7th and 3rd century BC in the area associated with the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacae" title="Sacae">Sacae</a></i>.</p>
<p>Ordinary Pazyryk graves contain only common utensils, but in one, among other treasures, archaeologists found the famous <a href="/wiki/File:Scythiancarpet.jpg" title="File:Scythiancarpet.jpg">Pazyryk Carpet</a>, the oldest surviving wool-pile <a href="/wiki/Oriental_rug" title="Oriental rug">oriental rug</a>. Another striking find, a 3-metre-high four-wheel funerary chariot, survived well-preserved from the 5th to 4th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although some scholars sought to connect the Pazyryk nomads with indigenous ethnic groups of the Altaic, Rudenko summed up the cultural context in the following observation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>All that is known to us at the present time about the culture of the population of the High Altai, who have left behind them the large cairns, permits us to refer them to the Scythian period, and the Pazyryk group in particular to the 5th century BC. This is supported by radiocarbon dating.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bilsk_excavations">Bilsk excavations</span></h3>
<p>Recent digs<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> (see:<a href="/wiki/Gelonus" title="Gelonus">Gelonus</a>) in a village <a href="/wiki/Bilsk,_Kotelva_Raion" title="Bilsk, Kotelva Raion">Bilsk</a> near <a href="/wiki/Poltava" title="Poltava">Poltava</a> (Ukraine) have uncovered a "vast city", with the largest area of any city in the world at that time (<a class="extiw" href="https://uk.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%91%D1%96%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BA%D0%B5_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%89%D0%B5" title="uk:Більське городище">Bilsk settlement</a>). It has been tentatively identified by a team of archaeologists led by <a href="/wiki/Boris_Shramko" title="Boris Shramko">Boris Shramko</a> as the site of <a href="/wiki/Gelonus" title="Gelonus">Gelonus</a>, the purported capital of Scythia. The city's commanding ramparts and vast area of 40 square kilometers exceed even the outlandish size reported by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>. Its location at the northern edge of the Ukrainian steppe would have allowed strategic control of the north-south <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a>-route. Judging by the finds dated to the 5th and 4th centuries BC, craft workshops and Greek pottery abounded.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tillia_Tepe_treasure">Tillia Tepe treasure</span></h3>
