<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tillia_Tepe" title="Tillia Tepe">Tillia Tepe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MenWithDragons.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="1190" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/MenWithDragons.jpg/220px-MenWithDragons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/MenWithDragons.jpg/330px-MenWithDragons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/MenWithDragons.jpg/440px-MenWithDragons.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MenWithDragons.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Kings with dragons", <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tillia_Tepe" title="Tillia Tepe">Tillia Tepe</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TilliaTepeCrown2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="387" data-file-width="456" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/TilliaTepeCrown2.jpg/220px-TilliaTepeCrown2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/TilliaTepeCrown2.jpg/330px-TilliaTepeCrown2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/TilliaTepeCrown2.jpg/440px-TilliaTepeCrown2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TilliaTepeCrown2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Royal crown, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tillia_Tepe" title="Tillia Tepe">Tillia Tepe</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A site found in 1968 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tillia_Tepe" title="Tillia Tepe">Tillia Tepe</a> (literally "the golden hill") in northern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> (former <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>) near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shebergan" title="Shebergan">Shebergan</a> consisted of the graves of five women and one man with extremely rich jewelry, dated to around the 1st century BC, and probably related to that of Scythian tribes normally living slightly to the north. Altogether the graves yielded several thousands of pieces of fine jewelry, usually made from combinations of <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turquoise" title="Turquoise">turquoise</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lapis-lazuli" title="Lapis-lazuli">lapis-lazuli</a>.</p>
<p>A high degree of cultural <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> pervades the findings, however. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> cultural and artistic influences appear in many of the forms and human depictions (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorini" title="Amorini">amorini</a> to rings with the depiction of <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> and her name inscribed in Greek), attributable to the existence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucid_empire" title="Seleucid empire">Seleucid empire</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian" title="Greco-Bactrian">Greco-Bactrian</a> kingdom in the same area until around 140 BC, and the continued existence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_kingdom" title="Indo-Greek kingdom">Indo-Greek kingdom</a> in the northwestern Indian sub-continent until the beginning of our era. This testifies to the richness of cultural influences in the area of <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> at that time.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_society">Culture and society</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tribal_divisions">Tribal divisions</span></h3>
<p>Scythians lived in <a href="/wiki/Confederation" title="Confederation">confederated</a> tribes, a political form of voluntary association which regulated pastures and organized a common defence against encroaching neighbors for the pastoral tribes of mostly <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">equestrian</a> herdsmen. While the productivity of domesticated animal-breeding greatly exceeded that of the settled agricultural societies, the pastoral economy also needed supplemental agricultural produce, and stable nomadic confederations developed either symbiotic or forced alliances with sedentary peoples – in exchange for animal produce and military protection.</p>
<p>Herodotus relates that three main tribes of the Scythians descended from three brothers, Lipoxais, Arpoxais, and Colaxais:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In their reign a <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">plough</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Yoke" title="Yoke">yoke</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Axe" title="Axe">axe</a>, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bowl_(vessel)" title="Bowl (vessel)">bowl</a>, all made of gold, fell from heaven upon the Scythian territory. The oldest of the brothers wished to take them away, but as he drew near the gold began to burn. The second brother approached them, but with the like result. The third and youngest then approached, upon which the fire went out, and he was enabled to carry away the golden gifts. The two eldest then made the youngest king, and henceforth the golden gifts were watched by the king with the greatest care, and annually approached with magnificent sacrifices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Herodotus also mentions a royal tribe or clan, an elite which dominated the other Scythians:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Then on the other side of the Gerros we have those parts which are called the "Royal" lands and those Scythians who are the bravest and most numerous and who esteem the other Scythians their slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The elder brothers then, acknowledging the significance of this thing, delivered the whole of the kingly power to the youngest. From Lixopais, they say, are descended those Scythians who are called the race of the Auchatai; from the middle brother Arpoxais those who are called Catiaroi and Traspians, and from the youngest of them the "Royal" tribe, who are called Paralatai: and the whole together are called, they say, Scolotoi, after the name of their king; but the Hellenes gave them the name of Scythians. Thus the Scythians say they were produced; and from the time of their origin, that is to say from the first king Targitaos, to the passing over of Dareios [the Persian Emperor <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a>] against them [512 BC], they say that there is a period of a thousand years and no more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ScytianBowl.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/ScytianBowl.JPG/220px-ScytianBowl.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/ScytianBowl.JPG/330px-ScytianBowl.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/ScytianBowl.JPG/440px-ScytianBowl.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ScytianBowl.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scythian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bowl_(vessel)" title="Bowl (vessel)">bowl</a>, 5th century BC found at <a href="/wiki/Castelu" title="Castelu">Castelu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>. In display at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constan%C5%A3a_Museum_of_National_History" title="Constanţa Museum of National History">Constanţa Museum of National History</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The rich burials of Scythian kings in <a href="/wiki/Tumulus" title="Tumulus">tumuli</a> (often known by the Turkic name <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurgans" title="Kurgans">kurgan</a></i>) is evidence for the existence of a powerful elite. While an elite clan is named in some classical sources<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2015)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> as the "Royal Dahae", the <a href="/wiki/Dahae" title="Dahae">Dahae</a> proper are generally regarded as an extinct Indo-European people, who occupied what is now Turkmenistan, and were distinct from the Scythians.</p>
<p>Although scholars have traditionally treated the three tribes as geographically distinct, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Dum%C3%A9zil" title="Georges Dumézil">Georges Dumézil</a> interpreted the divine gifts as the symbols of social occupations, illustrating his <a href="/wiki/Trifunctional_hypothesis" title="Trifunctional hypothesis">trifunctional vision</a> of early <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_society" title="Proto-Indo-European society">Indo-European</a> societies: the plough and yoke symbolised the farmers, the axe – the warriors, the bowl – the priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> According to Dumézil, "the fruitless attempts of Arpoxais and Lipoxais, in contrast to the success of Colaxais, may explain why the highest strata was not that of farmers or magicians, but rather that of warriors."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TillyaTepeSheath2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1487" data-file-width="3985" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/TillyaTepeSheath2.jpg/260px-TillyaTepeSheath2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/TillyaTepeSheath2.jpg/390px-TillyaTepeSheath2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/TillyaTepeSheath2.jpg/520px-TillyaTepeSheath2.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TillyaTepeSheath2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sheath for knives</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A warlike people, the Scythians were particularly known for their <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">equestrian</a> skills, and their early use of <a href="/wiki/Composite_bow" title="Composite bow">composite bows</a> shot from horseback. With great mobility, the Scythians could absorb the attacks of more cumbersome footsoldiers and cavalry, just retreating into the steppes. Such tactics wore down their enemies, making them easier to defeat. The Scythians were notoriously aggressive warriors. They "fought to live and lived to fight" and "drank the blood of their enemies and used the scalps as napkins."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parragon_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Parragon-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Ruled by small numbers of closely allied élites, Scythians had a reputation for their <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archers</a>, and many gained employment as <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenaries</a>. Scythian élites had <a href="/wiki/Kurgan" title="Kurgan">kurgan</a> tombs: high barrows heaped over chamber-tombs of <a href="/wiki/Larch" title="Larch">larch</a>-wood – a deciduous conifer that may have had special significance as a tree of life-renewal, for it stands bare in winter. Burials at <a href="/wiki/Pazyryk_burials" title="Pazyryk burials">Pazyryk</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altay_Mountains" title="Altay Mountains">Altay Mountains</a> have included some spectacularly preserved Scythians of the "Pazyryk culture" – including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pazyryk_Ice_Maiden" title="Pazyryk Ice Maiden">Ice Maiden</a> of the 5th century BC.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ziwiye_hoard" title="Ziwiye hoard">Ziwiye hoard</a>, a treasure of gold and silver metalwork and ivory found near the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakiz" title="Sakiz">Sakiz</a> south of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Urmia" title="Lake Urmia">Lake Urmia</a> and dated to between 680 and 625 BC, includes objects with Scythian "<a href="/wiki/Animal_style" title="Animal style">animal style</a>" features. One silver dish from this find bears some inscriptions, as yet undeciphered and so possibly representing a form of Scythian writing.</p>
<p>Scythians also had a reputation for the use of barbed and poisoned arrows of several types, for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomadic" title="Nomadic">nomadic</a> life centered on horses – "fed from horse-blood" according to Herodotus – and for skill in <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla warfare</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing">Clothing</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Scythians&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(December 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>According to Herodotus, Scythian costume consisted of padded and quilted leather trousers tucked into boots, and open tunics. They rode with no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stirrups" title="Stirrups">stirrups</a> or saddles, just saddle-cloths. Herodotus reports that Scythians used <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a>, both to weave their clothing and to cleanse themselves in its smoke (Hist. 4.73–75); archaeology has confirmed the use of cannabis in funeral rituals.</p>
<p>Scythian women dressed in much the same fashion as men. A Pazyryk burial, discovered in the 1990s, contained the skeletons of a man and a woman, each with weapons, arrowheads, and an axe. Herodotus mentioned that Sakas had "high caps and ... wore trousers." Clothing was sewn from plain-weave wool, hemp cloth, silk fabrics, felt, leather and hides.</p>
<p>Pazyryk findings give the most number of almost fully preserved garments and clothing worn by the Scythian/Saka peoples. Ancient Persian bas-relief – Apadana or Behistun inscription, ancient Greek pottery, archaeological findings from Ukraine, Russia, Kazakhstan, China et al. give visual representations of these garments.</p>
<p>Herodotus says Sakas had "high caps tapering to a point and stiffly upright." Asian Saka headgear is clearly visible on the Persepolis Apadana staircase bas-relief – high pointed hat with flaps over ears and the nape of the neck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saka_bas_relief_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-saka_bas_relief-118">[118]</a></sup> From China to the Danube delta, men seemed to have worn a variety of soft headgear – either conical like the one described by Herodotus, or rounder, more like a Phrygian cap.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:6._Pectorale_burial_mound_Arzhan_(VIII._-_VII._B._C.)_Tuva.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/6._Pectorale_burial_mound_Arzhan_%28VIII._-_VII._B._C.%29_Tuva.JPG/220px-6._Pectorale_burial_mound_Arzhan_%28VIII._-_VII._B._C.%29_Tuva.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/6._Pectorale_burial_mound_Arzhan_%28VIII._-_VII._B._C.%29_Tuva.JPG/330px-6._Pectorale_burial_mound_Arzhan_%28VIII._-_VII._B._C.%29_Tuva.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/6._Pectorale_burial_mound_Arzhan_%28VIII._-_VII._B._C.%29_Tuva.JPG/440px-6._Pectorale_burial_mound_Arzhan_%28VIII._-_VII._B._C.%29_Tuva.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:6._Pectorale_burial_mound_Arzhan_(VIII._-_VII._B._C.)_Tuva.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pectoral from burial mound in <a href="/wiki/Arzhan" title="Arzhan">Arzhan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Women wore a variety of different headdresses, some conical in shape others more like flattened cylinders, also adorned with metal (golden) plaques.</p>
<p>Based on the Pazyryk findings (can be seen also in the south Siberian, Uralic and Kazakhstan rock drawings) some caps were topped with zoomorphic wooden sculptures firmly attached to a cap and forming an integral part of the headgear, similar to the surviving nomad helmets from northern China. Men and warrior women wore tunics, often embroidered, adorned with felt applique work, or metal (golden) plaques.</p>
<p>Persepolis Apadana again serves a good starting point to observe tunics of the Sakas. They appear to be a sewn, long sleeve garment that extended to the knees and belted with a belt while owner's weapons were fastened to the belt (sword or dagger, <a href="/wiki/Gorytos" title="Gorytos">gorytos</a>, battleax, whetstone etc.). Based on numerous archeological findings in Ukraine, southern Russian and Kazakhstan men and warrior women wore long sleeve tunics that were always belted, often with richly ornamented belts. The Kazakhstan Saka (e.g. Issyk Golden Man/Maiden) wore shorter tunics and more close fitting tunics than the Pontic steppe Scythians. Some Pazyryk culture Saka wore short belted tunic with a lapel on a right side, upright collar, 'puffed' sleeves narrowing at a wrist and bound in narrow cuffs of a color different from the rest of the tunic.</p>
<p>Scythian women wore long, loose robes, ornamented with metal plaques (gold). Women wore shawls, often richly decorated with metal (golden) plaques.</p>
<p>Men and women wore coats, e.g. Pazyryk Saka had many varieties, from fur to felt. They could have worn a riding coat that later was known as a Median robe or Kantus. Long sleeved, and open, it seems that on the Persepolis Apadana Skudrian delegation is perhaps shown wearing such coat. The Pazyryk felt tapestry shows a rider wearing a billowing cloak.</p>
<p>Men and women wore long trousers, often adorned with metal plaques and often embroidered or adorned with felt appliqués; trousers could have been wider or tight fitting depending on the area. Materials used depended on the wealth, climate and necessity.</p>
<p>Men and women warriors wore variations of long and shorter boots, wool-leather-felt gaiter-boots and moccasin-like shoes. They were either of a laced or simple slip on type. Women wore also soft shoes with metal (gold) plaques.</p>
<p>Men and women wore belts. Warrior belts were made of leather, often with gold or other metal adornments and had many attached leather thongs for fastening of the owner's gorytos, sword, whet stone, whip etc. Belts were fastened with metal or horn <a href="/wiki/Belt_hook" title="Belt hook">belt-hooks</a>, leather thongs and metal (often golden) or horn belt-plates.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HorseAttackedByTigerOrdos4th-1stBCE.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1789" data-file-width="2609" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/HorseAttackedByTigerOrdos4th-1stBCE.JPG/220px-HorseAttackedByTigerOrdos4th-1stBCE.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/HorseAttackedByTigerOrdos4th-1stBCE.JPG/330px-HorseAttackedByTigerOrdos4th-1stBCE.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/HorseAttackedByTigerOrdos4th-1stBCE.JPG/440px-HorseAttackedByTigerOrdos4th-1stBCE.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HorseAttackedByTigerOrdos4th-1stBCE.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bronze <a href="/wiki/Ordos_culture" title="Ordos culture">Ordos culture</a> plaque, 4th century BC; a horse attacked by a tiger</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scythian_art" title="Scythian art">Scythian art</a></div>
<p>Scythian contacts with craftsmen in Greek colonies along the northern shores of the Black Sea resulted in the famous Scythian gold adornments that feature among the most glamorous artifacts of world museums. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnographically" title="Ethnographically">Ethnographically</a> extremely useful as well, the gold depicts Scythian men as bearded, long-haired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasoid" title="Caucasoid">Caucasoids</a>. "Greco-Scythian" works depicting Scythians within a much more <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Hellenic</a> style date from a later period, when Scythians had already adopted elements of Greek culture, and the most elaborate royal pieces are assumed to have been made by Greek goldsmiths for this lucrative market. Other metalwork pieces from across the whole Eurasian steppe use an <a href="/wiki/Animal_style" title="Animal style">animal style</a>, showing animals, often in combat and often with their legs folded beneath them. This origins of this style remain debated, but it probably both received and gave influences in the art of the neighbouring settled peoples, and acted as a fast route for transmission of motifs across the width of Eurasia.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Placca_pantera,_da_regione_di_krasnodar,_kurgan_chertomlyk,_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato,_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1555" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG/280px-Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG/420px-Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG/560px-Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Placca_pantera,_da_regione_di_krasnodar,_kurgan_chertomlyk,_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato,_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gold plaque with panther, probably for a shield or breast-plate, 13 in/33 cm long, end 7th-century BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Surviving Scythian objects are mostly small portable pieces of metalwork: elaborate personal jewelry, weapon-ornaments and horse-trappings. But finds from sites with <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> show rich and brightly coloured textiles, leatherwork and woodwork, not to mention tattooing. The western royal pieces executed Central-Asian animal motifs with Greek realism: winged <a href="/wiki/Griffin" title="Griffin">gryphons</a> attacking horses, battling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stag" title="Stag">stags</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>, combined with everyday motifs like milking <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">ewes</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChineseJadePlaques.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="953" data-file-width="978" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/ChineseJadePlaques.JPG/220px-ChineseJadePlaques.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/ChineseJadePlaques.JPG/330px-ChineseJadePlaques.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/ChineseJadePlaques.JPG/440px-ChineseJadePlaques.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChineseJadePlaques.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chinese <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steatite" title="Steatite">steatite</a> plaques, in the Scythian-style animal art of the steppes. 4th to 3rd centuries BC. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2000, the touring exhibition 'Scythian Gold' introduced the North American public to the objects made for Scythian nomads by Greek craftsmen north of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, and buried with their Scythian owners under burial mounds on the flat plains of present-day <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>. In 2001, the discovery of an undisturbed royal Scythian burial-barrow illustrated Scythian animal-style gold that lacks the direct influence of Greek styles. Forty-four pounds of gold weighed down the royal couple in this burial, discovered near <a href="/wiki/Kyzyl" title="Kyzyl">Kyzyl</a>, capital of the <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberian</a> republic of <a href="/wiki/Tuva" title="Tuva">Tuva</a>.</p>
<p>Ancient influences from Central Asia became identifiable in China following contacts of metropolitan China with nomadic western and northwestern border territories from the 8th century BC. The Chinese adopted the Scythian-style animal art of the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a> (descriptions of animals locked in combat), particularly the rectangular belt-plaques made of gold or bronze, and created their own versions in <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steatite" title="Steatite">steatite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following their expulsion by the <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a>, some Scythians may also have migrated to the area of <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> in southern China. Scythian warriors could also have served as mercenaries for the various kingdoms of ancient China. Excavations of the prehistoric art of the <a href="/wiki/Dian_Kingdom" title="Dian Kingdom">Dian</a> civilization of Yunnan have revealed hunting scenes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasoid" title="Caucasoid">Caucasoid</a> horsemen in Central Asian clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scythian influences have been identified as far as Korea and Japan. Various Korean artifacts, such as the royal crowns of the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a>, are said to be of Scythian design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Similar crowns, brought through contacts with the continent, can also be found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kofun_era" title="Kofun era">Kofun era</a> Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scythian_religion" title="Scythian religion">Scythian religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alba_Iulia_National_Museum_of_the_Union_2011_-_Offering_pot_from_a_Scythian_Grave.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Alba_Iulia_National_Museum_of_the_Union_2011_-_Offering_pot_from_a_Scythian_Grave.JPG/170px-Alba_Iulia_National_Museum_of_the_Union_2011_-_Offering_pot_from_a_Scythian_Grave.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Alba_Iulia_National_Museum_of_the_Union_2011_-_Offering_pot_from_a_Scythian_Grave.JPG/255px-Alba_Iulia_National_Museum_of_the_Union_2011_-_Offering_pot_from_a_Scythian_Grave.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Alba_Iulia_National_Museum_of_the_Union_2011_-_Offering_pot_from_a_Scythian_Grave.JPG/340px-Alba_Iulia_National_Museum_of_the_Union_2011_-_Offering_pot_from_a_Scythian_Grave.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alba_Iulia_National_Museum_of_the_Union_2011_-_Offering_pot_from_a_Scythian_Grave.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Offering pot from a Scythian grave from <a href="/wiki/Alba_Iulia" title="Alba Iulia">Alba Iulia</a>, Romania, 6th century BC. In display at <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_the_Union" title="National Museum of the Union">National Museum of the Union</a>, Alba Iulia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The religious beliefs of the Scythians was a type of Pre-Zoroastrian Iranian religion and differed from the post-Zoroastrian Iranian thoughts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-123">[123]</a></sup> Foremost in the Scythian pantheon stood <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabiti" title="Tabiti">Tabiti</a>, who was later replaced by <a href="/wiki/Atar" title="Atar">Atar</a>, the fire-pantheon of Iranian tribes, and <a href="/wiki/Agni" title="Agni">Agni</a>, the fire deity of Indo-Aryans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-123">[123]</a></sup> The Scythian belief was a more archaic stage than the <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> systems. The use of <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a> to induce trance and divination by soothsayers was a characteristic of the Scythian belief system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khotanese_animal_zodiac_BLI6_OR11252_1R2_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1107" data-file-width="862" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Khotanese_animal_zodiac_BLI6_OR11252_1R2_1.jpg/190px-Khotanese_animal_zodiac_BLI6_OR11252_1R2_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Khotanese_animal_zodiac_BLI6_OR11252_1R2_1.jpg/285px-Khotanese_animal_zodiac_BLI6_OR11252_1R2_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Khotanese_animal_zodiac_BLI6_OR11252_1R2_1.jpg/380px-Khotanese_animal_zodiac_BLI6_OR11252_1R2_1.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khotanese_animal_zodiac_BLI6_OR11252_1R2_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A document from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotan" title="Khotan">Khotan</a> written in <a href="/wiki/Saka_language" title="Saka language">Khotanese Saka</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Iranian_languages" title="Eastern Iranian languages">Eastern Iranian branch</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, listing the animals of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_zodiac" title="Chinese zodiac">Chinese zodiac</a> in the cycle of predictions for people born in that year; ink on paper, early 9th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scythian_languages" title="Scythian languages">Scythian languages</a></div>
<p>The Scythian group of languages in the early period are essentially unattested, and their internal divergence is difficult to judge. They belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Iranian_languages" title="Eastern Iranian languages">Eastern Iranian</a> family of languages. Whether all the peoples included in the "Scytho-Siberian" archaeological culture spoke languages from this family is uncertain.</p>
<p>The Scythian languages may have formed a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>: "Scytho-Sarmatian" in the west and "Scytho-Khotanese" or <a href="/wiki/Saka_language" title="Saka language">Saka</a> in the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Modern scholarly consensus is that the Saka language, ancestor to the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_languages" title="Pamir languages">Pamir languages</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_India" title="Northern India">northern India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saka_language" title="Saka language">Khotanese</a> in <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, China belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Scythian_languages" title="Scythian languages">Scythian languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The Scythian languages were mostly marginalized and assimilated as a consequence of the late antiquity and early Middle Ages <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_people" title="Slavic people">Slavic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkic_expansion" title="Turkic expansion">Turkic expansion</a>. Some remnants of the eastern groups have survived as modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashto_language" title="Pashto language">Pashto</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pamiri_languages" title="Pamiri languages">Pamiri languages</a> in Central Asia. The western (Sarmatian) group of ancient Scythian survived as the medieval language of the <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a> and eventually gave rise to the modern <a href="/wiki/Ossetian_language" title="Ossetian language">Ossetian language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Evidence of the Middle Iranian "Scytho-Khotanese" language survives in <a href="/wiki/Northwest_China" title="Northwest China">Northwest China</a>, where Khotanese-Saka-language documents, ranging from medical texts to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_literature" title="Buddhist literature">Buddhist literature</a>, have been found primarily in Khotan and <a href="/wiki/Tumxuk" title="Tumxuk">Tumshuq</a> (northeast of Kashgar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailey_1996_pp1231-1235_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-bailey_1996_pp1231-1235-127">[127]</a></sup> They largely <a href="/wiki/Islamicisation_and_Turkicisation_of_Xinjiang" title="Islamicisation and Turkicisation of Xinjiang">predate the arrival of Islam</a> to the region under the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a> <a href="/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate" title="Kara-Khanid Khanate">Kara-Khanids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailey_1996_pp1231-1235_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-bailey_1996_pp1231-1235-127">[127]</a></sup> Similar documents in the Khotanese-Saka language <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang_manuscripts" title="Dunhuang manuscripts">were found in Dunhuang</a> and date mostly from the 10th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span></h2>
<p>In artworks, the Scythians are portrayed exhibiting European traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a>, the 5th-century Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> describes the <a href="/wiki/Budini" title="Budini">Budini</a> of Scythia as <a href="/wiki/Red_hair" title="Red hair">red-haired</a> and grey-eyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup> In the 5th century BC, Greek physician <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> argued that the Scythians have purron (ruddy) skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> In the 3rd century BC, the Greek poet <a href="/wiki/Callimachus" title="Callimachus">Callimachus</a> described the Arismapes (<a href="/wiki/Arimaspi" title="Arimaspi">Arimaspi</a>) of Scythia as fair-haired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-3"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The 2nd century BC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_China" title="Han China">Han Chinese</a> envoy <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a> described the Sai (Scythians) as having yellow (probably meaning hazel or green), and blue eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-4"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a>, the 1st century AD Roman author <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> characterizes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seres" title="Seres">Seres</a>, sometimes identified as Iranians (Scythians) or <a href="/wiki/Tocharians" title="Tocharians">Tocharians</a>, as red-haired and blue-eyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-5"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> In the late 2nd century AD, the <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theologian</a> <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a> says that the Scythians are fair-haired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-6"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The 2nd century Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Polemon_of_Laodicea" title="Polemon of Laodicea">Polemon</a> includes the Scythians among the northern peoples characterized by red hair and blue-grey eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-7"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup> In the late 2nd or early 3rd century AD, the Greek physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> declares that Sarmatians, Scythians and other northern peoples have reddish hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-8"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The fourth-century Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a> wrote that the Alans, a people closely related to the Scythians, were tall, <a href="/wiki/Blond" title="Blond">blond</a> and light-eyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The 4th century <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishop</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nyssa_(Cappadocia)" title="Nyssa (Cappadocia)">Nyssa</a> <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nyssa" title="Gregory of Nyssa">Gregory of Nyssa</a> wrote that the Scythians were fair skinned and <a href="/wiki/Blond" title="Blond">blond</a> haired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The 5th-century physician <a href="/wiki/Adamantius" title="Adamantius">Adamantius</a>, who often follow Polemon, describes the Scythians are fair-haired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day55_129-9"><a href="#cite_note-Day55-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> It is possible that the later physical descriptions by Adamantius and Gregory of Scythians refer to <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">East Germanic tribes</a>, as the latter were frequently referred to as "Scythians" in Roman sources at that time.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Herodotus">Herodotus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items.JPG/220px-Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items.JPG/330px-Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items.JPG/440px-Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scythian artefacts originating from sites in <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvania</a>, in display at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aiud_History_Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aiud History Museum (page does not exist)">Aiud History Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aiud" title="Aiud">Aiud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Herodotus wrote about an enormous city, <a href="/wiki/Gelonus" title="Gelonus">Gelonus</a>, in the northern part of Scythia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>The Budini are a large and powerful nation: they have all deep blue eyes, and bright red hair. There is a city in their territory, called Gelonus, which is surrounded with a lofty wall, thirty furlongs (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">τριήκοντα σταδίων</span> = c. 5.5 km) each way, built entirely of wood. All the houses in the place and all the temples are of the same material. Here are temples built in honour of the Grecian gods, and adorned after the Greek fashion with images, altars, and shrines, all in wood. There is even a festival, held every third year in honour of Bacchus, at which the natives fall into the Bacchic fury. For the fact is that the Geloni were anciently Greeks, who, being driven out of the factories along the coast, fled to the Budini and took up their abode with them. They still speak a language half Greek, half Scythian.</dd>
</dl>
<p>Herodotus and other classical historians listed quite a number of tribes who lived near the Scythians, and presumably shared the same general milieu and nomadic steppe culture, often called "Scythian culture", even though scholars may have difficulties in determining their exact relationship to the "linguistic Scythians". A partial list of these tribes includes the <a href="/wiki/Agathyrsi" title="Agathyrsi">Agathyrsi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geloni" title="Geloni">Geloni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Budini" title="Budini">Budini</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neuri" title="Neuri">Neuri</a>.</p>
<p>Herodotus presented four different versions of Scythian origins:</p>
<ol>
<li>Firstly (4.7), the Scythians' legend about themselves, which portrays the first Scythian king, Targitaus, as the child of the sky-god and of a daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Dnieper" title="Dnieper">Dnieper</a>. Targitaus allegedly lived a thousand years before the failed Persian invasion of Scythia, or around 1500 BC. He had three sons, before whom fell from the sky a set of four golden implements – a plough, a yoke, a cup and a battle-axe. Only the youngest son succeeded in touching the golden implements without them bursting with fire, and this son's descendants, called by Herodotus the "Royal Scythians", continued to guard them.</li>
<li>Secondly (4.8), a legend told by the <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greeks</a> featuring Scythes, the first king of the Scythians, as a child of <a href="/wiki/Hercules" title="Hercules">Hercules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Echidna_(mythology)" title="Echidna (mythology)">Echidna</a>.</li>
<li>Thirdly (4.11), in the version which Herodotus said he believed most, the Scythians came from a more southern part of Central Asia, until a war with the <a href="/wiki/Massagetae" title="Massagetae">Massagetae</a> (a powerful tribe of steppe nomads who lived just northeast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>) forced them westward.</li>
<li>Finally (4.13), a legend which Herodotus attributed to the Greek bard <a href="/wiki/Aristeas" title="Aristeas">Aristeas</a>, who claimed to have got himself into such a Bachanalian fury that he ran all the way northeast across Scythia and further. According to this, the Scythians originally lived south of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urals" title="Urals">Rhipaean mountains</a>, until they got into a conflict with a tribe called the <a href="/wiki/Issedones" title="Issedones">Issedones</a>, pressed in their turn by the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclopes</a>; and so the Scythians decided to migrate westwards.</li>
</ol>
<p><a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a> and other peoples in Asia referred to the Scythians living in Asia as <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Sakas</a>. <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> (IV.64) describes them as Scythians, although they figure under a different name:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Sacae, or Scyths, were clad in trousers, and had on their heads tall stiff caps rising to a point. They bore the bow of their country and the dagger; besides which they carried the battle-axe, or <i><a href="/wiki/Sagaris" title="Sagaris">sagaris</a></i>. They were in truth Amyrgian (Western) Scythians, but the Persians called them Sacae, since that is the name which they gave to all Scythians.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Strabo">Strabo</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items-3.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items-3.JPG/220px-Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items-3.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items-3.JPG/330px-Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items-3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items-3.JPG/440px-Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items-3.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aiud_History_Museum_2011_-_Scythian_Items-3.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scythian artefacts originating from sites in <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvania</a>, in display at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aiud_History_Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aiud History Museum (page does not exist)">Aiud History Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aiud" title="Aiud">Aiud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1st century BC, the Greek-Roman geographer <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> gave an extensive description of the eastern Scythians, whom he located in Central Asia beyond <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strabo.2C_Geography.2C_11.8.1_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strabo.2C_Geography.2C_11.8.1-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>Strabo went on to list the names of the various tribes he believed to be "Scythian",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strabo.2C_Geography.2C_11.8.1_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strabo.2C_Geography.2C_11.8.1-139">[139]</a></sup> and in so doing almost certainly conflated them with unrelated tribes of eastern Central Asia.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Now the greater part of the Scythians, beginning at the Caspian Sea, are called <a href="/wiki/Dahae" title="Dahae">Däae</a>, but those who are situated more to the east than these are named <a href="/wiki/Massagetae" title="Massagetae">Massagetae</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakas" title="Sakas">Sacae</a>, whereas all the rest are given the general name of Scythians, though each people is given a separate name of its own. They are all for the most part nomads. But the best known of the nomads are those who <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">took away Bactriana</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greeks</a>, I mean the <a href="/wiki/Asii" title="Asii">Asii</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pasiani" title="Pasiani">Pasiani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bactria#Daxia.2C_Tukhara_.26_Tokharistan" title="Bactria">Tochari</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacaraucae" title="Sacaraucae">Sacarauli</a>, who originally came from the <a href="/wiki/Dayuan" title="Dayuan">country on the other side</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Iaxartes River</a> that adjoins that of the Sacae and the <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdiani</a> and was occupied by the Sacae. And as for the Däae, some of them are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aparni" title="Aparni">Aparni</a>, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xanthii" title="Xanthii">Xanthii</a>, and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pissuri" title="Pissuri">Pissuri</a>. Now of these the Aparni are situated closest to <a href="/wiki/Hyrcania" title="Hyrcania">Hyrcania</a> and the part of the sea that borders on it, but the remainder extend even as far as the country that stretches parallel to <a href="/wiki/Aria_(satrapy)" title="Aria (satrapy)">Aria</a>.</p>
<p>Between them and Hyrcania and <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a> and extending as far as the Arians is a <a href="/wiki/Karakum_Desert" title="Karakum Desert">great waterless desert</a>, which they traversed by long marches and then overran Hyrcania, <a href="/wiki/Nisa,_Turkmenistan" title="Nisa, Turkmenistan">Nesaea</a>, and the plains of the Parthians. And these people agreed to <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">pay tribute</a>, and the tribute was to allow the invaders at certain appointed times to overrun the country and carry off booty. But when the invaders overran their country more than the agreement allowed, war ensued, and in turn their quarrels were composed and new wars were begun. Such is the life of the other nomads also, who are always attacking their neighbors and then in turn settling their differences.</p>
</blockquote>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>(Strabo, <i>Geography</i>, 11.8.1; transl. 1903 by H.C. Hamilton &amp; W. Falconer.) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strabo.2C_Geography.2C_11.8.1_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-Strabo.2C_Geography.2C_11.8.1-139">[139]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_sources">Indian sources</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coin_of_Azes_II.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="860" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coin_of_Azes_II.jpg/220px-Coin_of_Azes_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coin_of_Azes_II.jpg/330px-Coin_of_Azes_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coin_of_Azes_II.jpg/440px-Coin_of_Azes_II.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coin_of_Azes_II.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silver coin of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Scythian" title="Indo-Scythian">Indo-Scythian</a> King <a href="/wiki/Azes_II" title="Azes II">Azes II</a> (ruled c. 35–12 BC). Note the royal tamga on the coin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythians</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Sakas</a> receive numerous mentions in Indian texts, including the <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manusmriti" title="Manusmriti">Manusmriti</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahabhashya" title="Mahabhashya">Mahabhashya</a> of <a href="/wiki/Patanjali" title="Patanjali">Patanjali</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-classical_.22Scythians.22">Post-classical "Scythians"</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration_period">Migration period</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ossetians" title="Ossetians">Ossetians</a></div>
<p>Although the classical Scythians may have largely disappeared by the 1st century BC, Eastern Romans continued to speak conventionally of "Scythians" to designate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Germanic_peoples" title="List of Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> tribes and confederations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> or mounted <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasian</a> nomadic barbarians in general: in AD 448 two mounted "Scythians" led the emissary <a href="/wiki/Priscus" title="Priscus">Priscus</a> to <a href="/wiki/Attila" title="Attila">Attila</a>'s encampment in <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a>. The Byzantines in this case carefully distinguished the Scythians from the Goths and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hun" title="Hun">Huns</a> who also followed Attila.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a> and finally the <a href="/wiki/Ossetians" title="Ossetians">Ossetians</a>) counted as Scythians in the broadest sense of the word – as speakers of Eastern Iranian languages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> and are considered mostly of <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Byzantine sources also refer to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rus%27_(people)" title="Rus' (people)">Rus</a> raiders who <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rus%27-Byzantine_War_(860)" title="Rus'-Byzantine War (860)">attacked Constantinople circa 860</a> in contemporary accounts as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tauroscythians" title="Tauroscythians">Tauroscythians</a>", because of their geographical origin, and despite their lack of any ethnic relation to Scythians. <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Patriarch Photius</a> may have first applied the term to them during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(860)" title="Siege of Constantinople (860)">Siege of Constantinople (860)</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Modern_usage">Early Modern usage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scythians_at_the_Tomb_of_Ovid_c._1640.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1016" data-file-width="842" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Scythians_at_the_Tomb_of_Ovid_c._1640.jpg/220px-Scythians_at_the_Tomb_of_Ovid_c._1640.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Scythians_at_the_Tomb_of_Ovid_c._1640.jpg/330px-Scythians_at_the_Tomb_of_Ovid_c._1640.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Scythians_at_the_Tomb_of_Ovid_c._1640.jpg/440px-Scythians_at_the_Tomb_of_Ovid_c._1640.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scythians_at_the_Tomb_of_Ovid_c._1640.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Scythians at the Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a></i> (c. 1640), by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Heinrich_Sch%C3%B6nfeld" title="Johann Heinrich Schönfeld">Johann Heinrich Schönfeld</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Owing to their reputation as established by Greek historians, the Scythians long served as the epitome of savagery and barbarism.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, in a letter ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul</a> "Scythian" is used as an example of people whom some label pejoratively, but who are, in Christ, acceptable to God:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Here there is no Greek or Jew. There is no difference between those who are circumcised and those who are not. There is no rude outsider, or even a Scythian. There is no slave or free person. But Christ is everything. And he is in everything.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>, for instance, alluded to the legend that Scythians ate their children in his play <i><a href="/wiki/King_Lear" title="King Lear">King Lear</a></i>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The barbarous <b>Scythian</b><br/></p>
<p>Or he that makes his generation messes<br/>
To gorge his appetite, shall to my bosom<br/>
¨ Be as well neighbour'd, pitied, and relieved,<br/>
As thou my <a href="/wiki/Cordelia" title="Cordelia">sometime daughter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Characteristically, early modern English discourse on Ireland frequently resorted to comparisons with Scythians in order to confirm that the indigenous population of Ireland descended from these ancient "bogeymen", and showed themselves as barbaric as their alleged ancestors. <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a> wrote that</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>the Chiefest [nation that settled in Ireland] I Suppose to be Scithians ... which firste inhabitinge and afterwarde stretchinge themselves forthe into the lande as theire numbers increased named it all of themselues Scuttenlande which more brieflye is Called Scuttlande or Scotlande.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>As proofs for this origin Spenser cites the alleged Irish customs of blood-drinking, nomadic lifestyle, the wearing of mantles and certain haircuts and</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Cryes allsoe vsed amongeste the Irishe which savor greatlye of the <i>Scythyan</i> Barbarisme.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/William_Camden" title="William Camden">William Camden</a>, one of Spenser's main sources, comments on this legend of origin that</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>to derive descent from a Scythian stock, cannot be thought any waies dishonourable, seeing that the Scythians, as they are most ancient, so they have been the Conquerours of most Nations, themselves alwaies invincible, and never subject to the Empire of others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%91%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="386" data-file-width="700" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/%D0%91%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg/260px-%D0%91%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/%D0%91%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg/390px-%D0%91%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/%D0%91%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg/520px-%D0%91%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%91%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%8F%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Romantic_nationalism" title="Romantic nationalism">Romantic nationalism</a>: <i>Battle between the Scythians and the Slavs</i> (<a href="/wiki/Viktor_Vasnetsov" title="Viktor Vasnetsov">Viktor Vasnetsov</a>, 1881).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 15th-century Polish chronicler <a href="/wiki/Jan_D%C5%82ugosz" title="Jan Długosz">Jan Długosz</a> was the first to connect the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Poland" title="Prehistory of Poland">prehistory of Poland</a> with Sarmatians, and the connection was taken up by other historians and chroniclers, such as <a href="/wiki/Marcin_Bielski" title="Marcin Bielski">Marcin Bielski</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcin_Kromer" title="Marcin Kromer">Marcin Kromer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maciej_Miechowita" title="Maciej Miechowita">Maciej Miechowita</a>. Other Europeans depended for their view of Polish <a href="/wiki/Sarmatism" title="Sarmatism">Sarmatism</a> on Miechowita's <i>Tractatus de Duabus Sarmatiis</i>, a work which provided a substantial source of information about the territories and peoples of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish-Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish-Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> in a language of international currency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarmatian_review_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-sarmatian_review-147">[147]</a></sup> Tradition specified that the Sarmatians themselves were descended from <a href="/wiki/Japheth" title="Japheth">Japheth</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 17th and 18th centuries, foreigners regarded the Russians as descendants of Scythians. It became conventional to refer to Russians as Scythians in 18th-century poetry, and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Blok" title="Alexander Blok">Alexander Blok</a> drew on this tradition sarcastically in his last major poem, <i>The Scythians</i> (1920). In the 19th century, romantic revisionists in the West transformed the "<a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarian</a>" Scyths of literature into the wild and free, hardy and democratic ancestors of all blond <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Indo-Europeans</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descent_claims">Descent claims</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Ovide_chez_les_Scythes_(1862).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2385" data-file-width="3821" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Ovide_chez_les_Scythes_%281862%29.jpg/260px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Ovide_chez_les_Scythes_%281862%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Ovide_chez_les_Scythes_%281862%29.jpg/390px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Ovide_chez_les_Scythes_%281862%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Ovide_chez_les_Scythes_%281862%29.jpg/520px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Ovide_chez_les_Scythes_%281862%29.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Ovide_chez_les_Scythes_(1862).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a>'s painting of the Roman poet, Ovid, in exile among the Scythians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parragon_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-Parragon-107">[107]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sarmatism" title="Sarmatism">Sarmatism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Generations_of_Noah" title="Generations of Noah">Generations of Noah</a></div>
<p>A number of groups have claimed possible descent from the Scythians, including the <a href="/wiki/Ossetians" title="Ossetians">Ossetians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashtun_people" title="Pashtun people">Pashtuns</a> (in particular, the <a href="/wiki/Sakzai" title="Sakzai">Sakzai</a> tribe) and the <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthians</a> (whose <a href="/wiki/Homeland" title="Homeland">homelands</a> lay to the east of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> and who were thought to have come there from north of the Caspian). Some legends of the <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Poles</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarmatian_review_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-sarmatian_review-147">[147]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaels</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hungarians" title="Hungarians">Hungarians</a> (in particular, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jassic_people" title="Jassic people">Jassics</a>), among others, also include mention of Scythian origins. Some writers claim that Scythians figured in the formation of the empire of the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> and likewise of <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_Albania" title="Caucasian Albania">Caucasian Albania</a>.</p>
<p>The Scythians also feature in some national origin-legends of the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a>. In the second paragraph of the 1320 <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Arbroath" title="Declaration of Arbroath">Declaration of Arbroath</a>, the élite of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> claim Scythia as a former homeland of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a>. According to the 11th-century <a href="/wiki/Lebor_Gab%C3%A1la_%C3%89renn" title="Lebor Gabála Érenn">Lebor Gabála Érenn</a> (<i>The Book of the Taking of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a></i>), the 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Auraicept_na_n-%C3%89ces" title="Auraicept na n-Éces">Auraicept na n-Éces</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irish_folklore" title="Irish folklore">Irish folklore</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a> originated in Scythia and were descendants of <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9nius_Farsaid" title="Fénius Farsaid">Fénius Farsaid</a>, a Scythian prince who created the <a href="/wiki/Ogham" title="Ogham">Ogham</a> alphabet and who was one of the principal architects of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaelic_language" title="Gaelic language">Gaelic language</a>.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingian" title="Carolingian">Carolingian</a> kings of the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> traced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merovingian" title="Merovingian">Merovingian</a> ancestry to the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> tribe of the <a href="/wiki/Sicambri" title="Sicambri">Sicambri</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a> documents in his <i>History of the Franks</i> that when <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis</a> was baptised, he was referred to as a Sicamber with the words "Mitis depone colla, Sicamber, adora quod incendisti, incendi quod adorasti." The <a href="/wiki/Chronicle_of_Fredegar" title="Chronicle of Fredegar">Chronicle of Fredegar</a> in turn reveals that the Franks believed the Sicambri to be a tribe of Scythian or Cimmerian descent, who had changed their name to <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> in honour of their chieftain Franco in 11 BC.</p>
<p>Based on such accounts of Scythian founders of certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" title="Germanic tribes">Germanic</a> as well as Celtic tribes, British historiography in the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> period such as <a href="/wiki/Sharon_Turner" title="Sharon Turner">Sharon Turner</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Anglo-Saxons" title="History of the Anglo-Saxons">History of the Anglo-Saxons</a></i>, made them the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>.</p>
<p>The idea was taken up in the <a href="/wiki/British_Israelism" title="British Israelism">British Israelism</a> of <a href="/wiki/John_Wilson_(historian)" title="John Wilson (historian)">John Wilson</a>, who adopted and promoted the idea that the "European Race, in particular the Anglo-Saxons, were descended from certain Scythian tribes, and these Scythian tribes (as many had previously stated from the Middle Ages onward) were in turn descended from the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Lost_Tribes" title="Ten Lost Tribes">Ten Lost Tribes</a> of Israel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Tudor Parfitt, author of The Lost Tribes of Israel and Professor of Modern Jewish Studies, points out that the proof cited by adherents of British Israelism is "of a feeble composition even by the low standards of the genre."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Geneticist Eran Elhaik believes the word <a href="/wiki/Ashkenaz" title="Ashkenaz">Ashkenaz</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Ashkenazi Jews</a>) is derived from the ancient Assyrian and Babylonian name for the Scythians. He places the original homeland of the Ashkenazi Jews in north-east Turkey and a region to the north of the Black sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-DavisKimball1995-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-DavisKimball1995_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-DavisKimball1995_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDavis-Kimball1995">Davis-Kimball (1995</a>, pp. 27–28)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/William_Watson_(sinologist)" title="William Watson (sinologist)">Watson, William</a>, "The Chinese Contribution to Eastern Nomad Culture in the Pre-Han and Early Han Periods", <i>World Archaeology</i>, Vol. 4, No. 2, Nomads (Oct., 1972), pp. 139–149, Taylor &amp; Francis, Ltd., <a class="external text" href="http://www.jstor.org/stable/123972" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Di Cosimo, Nicola, "The Northern Frontier in Pre-Imperial China (1,500 – 221 BC)", in: M. Loeuwe, E.L. Shaughnessy, eds, <i>The Cambridge History of Ancient China: From the Origins of Civilization to 221BC</i>, 1999, Cambridge University Press 1999, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780521470308">ISBN 9780521470308</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://s155239215.onlinehome.us/turkic/btn_Archeology/Zhou/CambridgeZhouChouNorthChinatEn.htm" rel="nofollow">online text</a>, "Even though there were fundamental ways in which nomadic groups over such a vast territory differed, the terms “Scythian” and “Scythic” have been widely adopted to describe a special phase that followed the widespread diffusion of mounted <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadism</a>, characterized by the presence of special weapons, horse gear, and animal art in the form of metal plaques. Archaeologists have used the term “Scythic continuum” in a broad cultural sense to indicate the early nomadic cultures of the Eurasian steppe. The term “Scythic” draws attention to the fact that there are elements – shapes of weapons, vessels, and ornaments, as well as lifestyle – common to both the eastern and the western ends of the Eurasian steppe region."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Taylor_.26_Francis-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Taylor_.26_Francis_30-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Taylor_.26_Francis_30-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Adams, Douglas Q. (1997). <i>Encyclopedia of Indo-European Culture</i>. Taylor &amp; Francis. p. 523. <q>(..) In their Ukrainian and Polish homeland the Slavs were intermixed and at times overlain by Germanic speakers (the Goths) and by Iranian speakers (Scythians, Sarmatians, Alans) in a shifting array of tribal and national configurations.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AScythians&amp;rft.aufirst=Douglas+Q.&amp;rft.aulast=Adams&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopedia+of+Indo-European+Culture&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=523&amp;rft.pub=Taylor+%26+Francis&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Women_in_Russia-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Women_in_Russia_31-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Women_in_Russia_31-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Atkinson, Dorothy; et al. (1977). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=rwarAAAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA3&amp;lpg=PA3" rel="nofollow"><i>Women in Russia</i></a>. Stanford University Press. p. 3. <q>(..) Ancient accounts link the Amazons with the Scythians and the Sarmatians, who successively dominated the south of Russia for a millennium extending back to the seventh century B.C. The descendants of these peoples were absorbed by the Slavs who came to be known as Russians.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AScythians&amp;rft.aufirst=Dorothy&amp;rft.aulast=Atkinson&amp;rft.btitle=Women+in+Russia&amp;rft.date=1977&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DrwarAAAAIAAJ%26pg%3DPA3%26lpg%3DPA3&amp;rft.pages=3&amp;rft.pub=Stanford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Slovene_Studies-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Slovene_Studies_32-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Slovene_Studies_32-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><i>Slovene Studies</i>. <b>9–11</b>. Society for Slovene Studies. 1987. p. 36. <q>(..) <li id="cite_note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Ossetes, the only Iranian people presently<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Scythians&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> resident in Europe, call their country <i>Iriston</i> or <i>Iron</i>, though <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Ossetia" title="North Ossetia">North Ossetia</a> now<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Scythians&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> officially has the designation <i><a href="/wiki/Alania" title="Alania">Alania</a></i>. They speak an Eastern Iranian language <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ossetic" title="Ossetic">Ossetic</a>, whose more widely spoken dialect, <i>Iron</i> or <i>Ironig</i> (i.e. Iranian), preserves some similarities with the <a href="/wiki/Gathas" title="Gathas">Gathic</a> <a href="/wiki/Avestan" title="Avestan">Avestan</a> language, another Iranian language of the Eastern branch</span></li>

<li>Johnson, James William, "The Scythian: His Rise and Fall", <i>Journal of the History of Ideas</i>, Vol. 20, No. 2 (Apr., 1959), pp. 250–257, University of Pennsylvania Press, <a class="external text" href="http://www.jstor.org/stable/2707822" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></li>

<li>Lebedynsky Iaroslav (2006) "Les Saces", Editions Errance, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/2877723372">ISBN 2-87772-337-2</a></li>
<li>Mallory, J.P. (1989). <i>In Search of the Indo-Europeans: Language Archeology and Myth</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thames_and_Hudson" title="Thames and Hudson">Thames and Hudson</a>. Chapter 2; and pages 51–53 for a quick reference.</li>
<li>Newark, T. (1985). <i>The Barbarians: Warriors and wars of the Dark Ages</i>. Blandford: New York. See pages 65, 85, 87, 119–139.</li>
<li>Renfrew, C. (1988). <i>Archeology and Language: The Puzzle of Indo-European origins</i>. Cambridge University Press.</li>
<li>Rolle, Renate, <i>The world of the Scythians</i>, London and New York (1989).</li>
<li><span class="languageicon" style="font-size:0.95em; font-weight:bold; color:#555;">(Russian)</span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rybakov,_Boris" title="Rybakov, Boris">Rybakov, Boris</a>. <i>Paganism of Ancient Rus</i>. Nauka, Moscow, 1987</li>
<li>Torday, Laszlo (1998). <i>Mounted Archers: The Beginnings of Central Asian History</i>. Durham Academic Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1900838036">ISBN 1-900838-03-6</a>.</li>
